<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><i><b>Mulholland Drive</b></i> (stylized as <i><b>Mulholland Dr.</b></i>) is a 2001 <a href="/wiki/Neo-noir" title="Neo-noir">neo-noir</a> <a href="/wiki/Mystery_film" title="Mystery film">mystery film</a> written and directed by <a href="/wiki/David_Lynch" title="David Lynch">David Lynch</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Justin_Theroux" title="Justin Theroux">Justin Theroux</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Watts" title="Naomi Watts">Naomi Watts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Harring" title="Laura Harring">Laura Harring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ann_Miller" title="Ann Miller">Ann Miller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Forster" title="Robert Forster">Robert Forster</a>. It tells the story of an aspiring actress named Betty Elms (Watts), newly arrived in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, who meets and befriends an <a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesiac</a> woman (Harring) hiding in an apartment which belongs to Betty's aunt. The story includes several other seemingly unrelated <a href="/wiki/Vignette_(literature)" title="Vignette (literature)">vignettes</a> that eventually interlock, as well as some surreal and darkly comic scenes and images that relate to the cryptic narrative.</p>
<p>Originally conceived as a <a href="/wiki/Television_pilot" title="Television pilot">television pilot</a>, a large portion of the film was shot in 1999 with Lynch's plan to keep it open-ended for a potential series. After viewing Lynch's version, however, television executives rejected it. Lynch then provided an ending to the project, making it a feature film. The half-pilot, half-feature result, along with Lynch's characteristic style, has left the general meaning of the film's events open to interpretation. Lynch has declined to offer an explanation of his intentions for the narrative, leaving audiences, critics, and cast members to speculate on what transpires. He gave the film the tagline "A love story in the city of dreams".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Categorized as a <a href="/wiki/Psychological_thriller" title="Psychological thriller">psychological thriller</a>, the film was acclaimed by critics and earned Lynch the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prix_de_la_mise_en_sc%C3%A8ne" title="Prix de la mise en scÃ¨ne">Prix de la mise en scÃ¨ne</a></i> (Best Director Award) at the <a href="/wiki/2001_Cannes_Film_Festival" title="2001 Cannes Film Festival">2001 Cannes Film Festival</a>, as well as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Oscar</a> nomination for <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Director" title="Academy Award for Best Director">Best Director</a>. <i>Mulholland Drive</i> launched the careers of Watts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and Harring and was the last feature film to star veteran Hollywood actress <a href="/wiki/Ann_Miller" title="Ann Miller">Ann Miller</a>. It is now widely regarded as one of Lynch's finest works, and as one of the greatest films of the 21st century. <a href="/wiki/A._O._Scott" title="A. O. Scott">A. O. Scott</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> writes that while some might consider the plot an "offense against narrative orderÂ ... the film is an intoxicating liberation from sense, with moments of feeling all the more powerful for seeming to emerge from the murky night world of the unconscious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Plot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A dark-haired woman is the sole survivor of a car accident on <a href="/wiki/Mulholland_Drive" title="Mulholland Drive">Mulholland Drive</a>, a winding road high in the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Hills" title="Hollywood Hills">Hollywood Hills</a>. Injured and in shock, she makes her way down into Los Angeles and sneaks into an apartment. Later that morning, an aspiring actress named Betty Elms arrives at the apartment, which is normally occupied by her aunt. Betty is startled to find the woman, who has amnesia and assumes the name "Rita" after seeing a poster for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Gilda" title="Gilda">Gilda</a></i> starring <a href="/wiki/Rita_Hayworth" title="Rita Hayworth">Rita Hayworth</a>. To help the woman remember her identity, Betty looks in Rita's purse, where she finds a large amount of money and an unusual blue key.</p>
<p>At a diner called Winkie's, a man tells his companion about a nightmare in which he dreamt there was a horrible figure behind the diner. When they investigate, the figure appears, causing the man with the nightmare to collapse in fright. Elsewhere, director Adam Kesher has his film commandeered by mobsters, who insist that he cast an unknown actress named Camilla Rhodes as the lead in his film. Kesher resists, but after being thrown out of his house when he finds his wife cheating on him, he learns that his bank has closed his line of credit and he is broke. He agrees to meet a mysterious figure called The Cowboy, who urges him to cast Camilla Rhodes for his own good. Later, a bungling hit man attempts to steal a book full of phone numbers and leaves three people dead.</p>
<p>While trying to learn more about Rita's accident, Betty and Rita go to Winkie's and are served by a waitress named Diane, which causes Rita to remember the name "Diane Selwyn". They find Diane Selwyn in the phone book and call her, but she does not answer. Betty goes to an audition, where her performance is highly praised. A casting agent takes her to a soundstage where a film called <i>The Sylvia North Story</i>, directed by Kesher, is being cast. When Camilla Rhodes auditions, Kesher capitulates to casting her. Betty locks eyes with Adam, but she flees before she can meet him, saying that she is late to meet a friend.</p>
<p>Betty and Rita go to Diane Selwyn's apartment and break in when no one answers the door. In the bedroom they find the body of a woman who has been dead for several days. Terrified, they return to their apartment, where Rita disguises herself with a blonde wig. She and Betty make love. At 2 a.m., Rita insists that they go to a theater called Club Silencio. The emcee explains in several languages that everything is an illusion; <a href="/wiki/Rebekah_Del_Rio" title="Rebekah Del Rio">Rebekah Del Rio</a> comes on stage and begins singing the <a href="/wiki/Roy_Orbison" title="Roy Orbison">Roy Orbison</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Crying_(Roy_Orbison_song)" title="Crying (Roy Orbison song)">Crying</a>" in Spanish, then collapses, while her vocals continue â the performance was a recording. Betty finds a blue box in her purse that matches Rita's key. Upon returning to the apartment, Rita retrieves the key and finds that Betty has disappeared. Rita unlocks the box, and it falls to the floor with a thump.</p>
<p>Diane Selwyn wakes up in her bed in the same apartment that Betty and Rita investigated. She looks exactly like Betty, but is a failed actress driven into a deep depression by her failed affair with Camilla Rhodes, who looks exactly like Rita. On Camilla's invitation, Diane attends a party at Adam's house on Mulholland Drive. At dinner, Diane states that she came to Hollywood when her aunt died, and met Camilla at an audition for <i>The Sylvia North Story</i>. Another woman kisses Camilla, and they turn and smile at Diane. Adam and Camilla prepare to make an important announcement, and dissolve into laughter and then kiss while Diane watches, crying.</p>
<p>In her apartment, Diane finds a blue key on her coffee tableâa key that the hit man had told her she would find when he had finished killing Camilla for her. Distraught, she is terrorized by hallucinations. She runs screaming to her bed, where she shoots herself. A woman at the club whispers "Silencio".</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Originally conceived as a television series, <i>Mulholland Drive</i> began as a 90-minute pilot produced for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touchstone_Television" title="Touchstone Television">Touchstone Television</a> and intended for the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> television network. David Lynch sold the idea to ABC executives based only on the story of Rita emerging from the car accident with her purse containing $125,000 in cash and the blue key, and Betty trying to help her figure out who she is. An ABC executive recalled, "I remember the creepiness of this woman in this horrible, horrible crash, and David teasing us with the notion that people are chasing her. She's not just 'in' troubleâshe <i>is</i> trouble. Obviously, we asked, 'What happens next?' And David said, 'You have to buy the pitch for me to tell you.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000206_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000206-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lynch described the attractiveness of the idea of a pilot, despite the knowledge that the medium of television would be constricting: "I'm a sucker for a continuing storyÂ ... Theoretically, you can get a very deep story and you can go so deep and open the world so beautifully, but it takes time to do that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divine_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-divine-7">[7]</a></sup> The story included normal and <a href="/wiki/Surrealist_cinema" title="Surrealist cinema">surreal</a> elements, much like Lynch's earlier series <i><a href="/wiki/Twin_Peaks" title="Twin Peaks">Twin Peaks</a></i>. Groundwork was laid for <a href="/wiki/Story_arc" title="Story arc">story arcs</a>, such as the mystery of Rita's identity, Betty's career and Adam Kesher's film project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000205.E2.80.93214_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000205.E2.80.93214-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Actress <a href="/wiki/Sherilyn_Fenn" title="Sherilyn Fenn">Sherilyn Fenn</a> stated in a 2014 interview that the original idea came during the filming of <i>Twin Peaks</i>, as a <a href="/wiki/Spin-off_(media)" title="Spin-off (media)">spin-off</a> film for her character of <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Horne" title="Audrey Horne">Audrey Horne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherilyn_Fenn_AV_Club_Interview_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherilyn_Fenn_AV_Club_Interview-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casting">Casting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Casting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lynch cast <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Watts" title="Naomi Watts">Naomi Watts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laura_Harring" title="Laura Harring">Laura Harring</a> by their photographs. He called them in separately for half-hour interviews and told them that he had not seen any of their previous works in film or television.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-david-10">[10]</a></sup> Harring considered it fateful that she was involved in a minor car accident on the way to the first interview, only to learn her character would also be involved in a car accident in the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Watts arrived wearing jeans for the first interview, direct from the airplane from New York City. Lynch asked her to return the next day "more glammed up". She was offered the part two weeks later. Lynch explained his selection of Watts, "I saw someone that I felt had a tremendous talent, and I saw someone who had a beautiful soul, an intelligenceâpossibilities for a lot of different roles, so it was a beautiful full package."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheng_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-cheng-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Justin_Theroux" title="Justin Theroux">Justin Theroux</a> also met Lynch directly after his airplane flight. After a long flight with little sleep, Theroux arrived dressed all in black, with untidy hair. Lynch liked the look and decided to cast Adam wearing similar clothes and the same hairstyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neman_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-neman-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filming">Filming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Filming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>One night, I sat down, the ideas came in, and it was a most beautiful experience. Everything was seen from a different angleÂ ... Now, looking back, I see that [the film] always wanted to be this way. It just took this strange beginning to cause it to be what it is.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">David Lynch, 2001</cite></div>
<p>Filming for the television pilot began on location in Los Angeles in February 1999 and took six weeks. Ultimately, the network was unhappy with the pilot and decided not to place it on its schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000213.E2.80.93214_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000213.E2.80.93214-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Objections included the nonlinear storyline, the ages of Harring and Watts (whom they considered too old), cigarette smoking by Ann Miller's character and a close-frame shot of dog feces in one scene. Lynch remembered, "All I know is, I loved making it, ABC hated it, and I don't like the cut I turned in. I agreed with ABC that the longer cut was too slow, but I was forced to butcher it because we had a deadline, and there wasn't time to finesse anything. It lost texture, big scenes and storylines, and there are 300 tape copies of the bad version circulating around. Lots of people have seen it, which is embarrassing, because they're bad-quality tapes, too. I don't want to think about it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000214_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000214-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The script was later rewritten and expanded when Lynch decided to transform it into a feature film. Describing the transition from an open-ended pilot to a feature film with a resolution of sorts, Lynch said, "One night, I sat down, the ideas came in, and it was a most beautiful experience. Everything was seen from a different angleÂ ... Now, looking back, I see that [the film] always wanted to be this way. It just took this strange beginning to cause it to be what it is."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup> The result was an extra eighteen pages of material that included the romantic relationship between Rita and Betty and the events that occurred after the blue box was opened. Watts was relieved that the pilot was dropped by ABC. She found Betty too one-dimensional without the darker portion of the film that was put together afterward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-17">[17]</a></sup> Most of the new scenes were filmed in October 2000, funded with $7Â million from French <a href="/wiki/Production_company" title="Production company">production company</a> <a href="/wiki/StudioCanal" title="StudioCanal">StudioCanal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-david-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Theroux described approaching filming without entirely understanding what the plot was about: "You get the whole script, but he might as well withhold the scenes you're not in, because the whole turns out to be more mystifying than the parts. David welcomes questions, but he won't answer any of themÂ ... You work kind of half-blindfolded. If he were a first-time director and hadn't demonstrated any command of this method, I'd probably have reservations. But it obviously works for him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timestheroux_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-timestheroux-18">[18]</a></sup> Theroux noted that the only answer Lynch did provide was that he was certain that Theroux's character, a Hollywood director, was not autobiographical of Lynch. Watts stated that she tried to bluff Lynch by pretending she had the plot figured out, and that he delighted in the cast's frustration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-david-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes_and_interpretations">Themes and interpretations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Themes and interpretations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Giving the film only the tagline "A love story in the city of dreams",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup> David Lynch has refused to comment on <i>Mulholland Drive</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s meaning or <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolism</a>, leading to much discussion and multiple interpretations. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Science_Monitor" title="The Christian Science Monitor">The Christian Science Monitor</a></i> film critic <a href="/wiki/David_Sterritt" title="David Sterritt">David Sterritt</a> spoke with Lynch after the film screened at Cannes and wrote that the director "insisted that <i>Mulholland Drive</i> does tell a coherent, comprehensible story", unlike some of Lynch's earlier films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> On the other hand, Justin Theroux said of Lynch's feelings on the multiple meanings people perceive in the film, "I think he's genuinely happy for it to mean anything you want. He loves it when people come up with really bizarre interpretations. David works from his subconscious."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timestheroux_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-timestheroux-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dreams_and_alternative_realities">Dreams and alternative realities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dreams and alternative realities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An early interpretation of the film uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dream_analysis" title="Dream analysis">dream analysis</a> to explain that the first part is a dream of the real Diane Selwyn, who has cast her dream-self as the innocent and hopeful "Betty Elms", reconstructing her history and persona into something like an old Hollywood film. In the dream, Betty is successful, charming, and lives the fantasy life of a soon-to-be-famous actress. The last one-fifth of the film presents Diane's real life, in which she has failed both personally and professionally. She arranges for Camilla, an ex-lover, to be killed, and unable to cope with the guilt, re-imagines her as the dependent, pliable amnesiac Rita. Clues to her inevitable demise, however, continue to appear throughout her dream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tang_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-tang-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>This interpretation was similar to what Naomi Watts construed, when she said in an interview, "I thought Diane was the real character and that Betty was the person she wanted to be and had dreamed up. Rita is the <a href="/wiki/Damsel_in_distress" title="Damsel in distress">damsel in distress</a> and she's in absolute need of Betty, and Betty controls her as if she were a doll. Rita is Betty's fantasy of who she wants Camilla to be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-17">[17]</a></sup> Watts' own early experiences in Hollywood parallel those of Diane's. She endured some professional frustration before she became successful, auditioned for parts in which she did not believe, and encountered people who did not follow through with opportunities. She recalled, "There were a lot of promises, but nothing actually came off. I ran out of money and became quite lonely."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pearce_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-pearce-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> asked six well-known film critics for their own perceptions of the overall meaning in <i>Mulholland Drive</i>. Neil Roberts of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i> and Tom Charity of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_Out_(magazine)" title="Time Out (magazine)">Time Out</a></i> subscribe to the theory that Betty is Diane's projection of a happier life. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Ross" title="Jonathan Ross">Jonathan Ross</a> seem to accept this interpretation, but both hesitate to overanalyze the film. Ebert states, "There is no explanation. There may not even be a mystery." Ross observes that there are storylines that go nowhere: "Perhaps these were leftovers from the pilot it was originally intended to be, or perhaps these things are the <a href="/wiki/Non_sequitur_(literary_device)" title="Non sequitur (literary device)">non-sequiturs</a> and subconscious of dreams."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_French" title="Philip French">Philip French</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> sees it as an allusion to Hollywood tragedy, while Jane Douglas from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">BBC</a> rejects the theory of Betty's life as Diane's dream, but also warns against too much analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another theory offered is that the narrative is a <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6bius_strip" title="MÃ¶bius strip">MÃ¶bius strip</a>, a twisted band that has no beginning and no end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudson_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-23">[23]</a></sup> In another interpretation, Betty and Rita and Diane and Camilla may exist in <a href="/wiki/Multiverse" title="Multiverse">parallel universes</a> that sometimes interconnect. Or the entire film is a dream, but whose dream is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapote_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-lapote-24">[24]</a></sup> Repeated references to beds, bedrooms and sleeping symbolize the heavy influence of dreams. Rita falls asleep several times; in between these episodes, disconnected scenes such as the men having a conversation at Winkie's, Betty's arrival in Los Angeles and the bungled hit take place, suggesting that Rita may be dreaming them. The opening shot of the film zooms into a bed containing an unknown sleeper, instilling, according to film scholar Ruth Perlmutter, the necessity to ask if what follows is reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-25">[25]</a></sup> Professor of dream studies Kelly Bulkeley argues that the early scene at the diner, as the only one in which dreams or dreaming are explicitly mentioned, illustrates "revelatory truth and <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a> uncertainty in Lynch's film".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreams_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dreams-26">[26]</a></sup> The monstrous being from the dream, who is the subject of conversation of the men in Winkie's, reappears at the end of the film right before and after Diane commits suicide. Bulkeley asserts that the lone discussion of dreams in that scene presents an opening to "a new way of understanding everything that happens in the movie".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreams_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dreams-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Philosopher and film theorist Robert Sinnerbrink similarly notes that the images following Diane's apparent suicide undermine the "dream and reality" interpretation. After Diane shoots herself, the bed is consumed with smoke, and Betty and Rita are shown beaming at each other, after which a woman in the Club Silencio balcony whispers "<i>Silencio</i>" as the screen fades to black. Sinnerbrink writes that the "concluding images float in an indeterminate zone between fantasy and reality, which is perhaps the genuinely metaphysical dimension of the cinematic image", also noting that it might be that the "last sequence comprises the fantasy images of Diane's dying consciousness, concluding with the real moment of her death: the final <i>Silencio</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sinnerbrink_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-sinnerbrink-27">[27]</a></sup> Referring to the same sequence, film theorist Andrew Hageman notes that "the ninety-second coda that follows Betty/Diane's suicide is a cinematic space that persists after the curtain has dropped on her living consciousness, and this persistent space is the very theatre where the illusion of illusion is continually unmasked".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hageman_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-hageman-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Film theorist David Roche writes that Lynch films do not simply tell detective stories, but rather force the audience into the role of becoming detectives themselves to make sense of the narratives, and that <i>Mulholland Drive</i>, like other Lynch films, frustrates "the spectator's need for a rational <a href="/wiki/Diegesis" title="Diegesis">diegesis</a> by playing on the spectator's mistake that narration is synonymous with diegesis". In Lynch's films, the spectator is always "one step behind narration" and thus "narration prevails over diegesis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roche_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roche-29">[29]</a></sup> Roche also notes that there are multiple mysteries in the film that ultimately go unanswered by the characters who meet dead ends, like Betty and Rita, or give in to pressures as Adam does. Although the audience still struggles to make sense of the stories, the characters are no longer trying to solve their mysteries. Roche concludes that <i>Mulholland Drive</i> is a mystery film not because it allows the audience to view the solution to a question, but the film itself is a mystery that is held together "by the spectator-detective's desire to make sense" of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roche_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roche-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_.22poisonous_valentine_to_Hollywood.22">A "poisonous valentine to Hollywood"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title='Edit section: A "poisonous valentine to Hollywood"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regardless of the proliferation of theories, critics note that no explanation satisfies all of the loose ends and questions that arise from the film. Stephen Holden of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> writes, "<i>Mulholland Drive</i> has little to do with any single character's love life or professional ambition. The movie is an ever-deepening reflection on the allure of Hollywood and on the multiple role-playing and self-invention that the movie-going experience promisesÂ ... What greater power is there than the power to enter and to program the dream life of the culture?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holden_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-holden-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._Hoberman" title="J. Hoberman">J. Hoberman</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> echoes this sentiment by calling it a "poisonous valentine to Hollywood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoberman_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoberman-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:40%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Contained within the original DVD release is a card titled "David Lynch's 10 Clues to Unlocking This Thriller". The clues are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Pay particular attention in the beginning of the film: At least two clues are revealed before the credits.</li>
<li>Notice appearances of the red lampshade.</li>
<li>Can you hear the title of the film that Adam Kesher is auditioning actresses for? Is it mentioned again?</li>
<li>An accident is a terrible eventânotice the location of the accident.</li>
<li>Who gives a key, and why?</li>
<li>Notice the robe, the ashtray, the coffee cup.</li>
<li>What is felt, realized and gathered at the Club Silencio?</li>
<li>Did talent alone help Camilla?</li>
<li>Note the occurrences surrounding the man behind Winkie's.</li>
<li>Where is Aunt Ruth?</li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">2002 DVD edition insert<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p><i>Mulholland Drive</i> has been compared with <a href="/wiki/Billy_Wilder" title="Billy Wilder">Billy Wilder</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Film_noir" title="Film noir">film noir</a> classic <i><a href="/wiki/Sunset_Boulevard_(film)" title="Sunset Boulevard (film)">Sunset Boulevard</a></i> (1950), another tale about broken dreams in Hollywood,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheenDavison2004170_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheenDavison2004170-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vass_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-vass-34">[34]</a></sup> and early in the film Rita is shown crossing Sunset Boulevard at night. Apart from both titles being named after iconic Los Angeles streets, <i>Mulholland Drive</i> is "Lynch's unique account of what held Wilder's attention too: human <a href="/wiki/Putrefaction" title="Putrefaction">putrefaction</a> (a term Lynch used several times during his press conference at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Film_Festival" title="New York Film Festival">New York Film Festival</a> 2001) in a city of lethal illusions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup> The title of the film is a reference to iconic Hollywood culture. David Lynch lives near Mulholland Drive, and stated in an interview, "At night, you ride on the top of the world. In the daytime you ride on top of the world, too, but it's mysterious, and there's a hair of fear because it goes into remote areas. You feel the history of Hollywood in that road."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup> Watts also had experience with the road before her career was established: "I remember driving along the street many times sobbing my heart out in my car, going, 'What am I doing here?<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheng_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-cheng-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critic Gregory Weight cautions viewers against a cynical interpretation of the events in the film, stating that Lynch presents more than "the faÃ§ade and that he believes only evil and deceit lie beneath it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weight_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-weight-36">[36]</a></sup> As much as Lynch makes a statement about the deceit, manipulation and false pretenses in Hollywood culture, he also infuses <a href="/wiki/Nostalgia" title="Nostalgia">nostalgia</a> throughout the film and recognizes that real art comes from classic filmmaking as Lynch cast thereby paying tribute to veteran actors <a href="/wiki/Ann_Miller" title="Ann Miller">Ann Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Grant" title="Lee Grant">Lee Grant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chad_Everett" title="Chad Everett">Chad Everett</a>. He also portrays Betty as extraordinarily talented and that her abilities are noticed by powerful people in the entertainment industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weight_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-weight-36">[36]</a></sup> Commenting on the contrasting positions between film nostalgia and the putrefaction of Hollywood, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Dillon_(writer_and_professor)" title="Steven Dillon (writer and professor)">Steven Dillon</a> writes that <i>Mulholland Drive</i> is critical of the culture of Hollywood as much as it is a condemnation of "<a href="/wiki/Cinephilia" title="Cinephilia">cinephilia</a>" (the fascination of filmmaking and the fantasies associated with it).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon200694_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon200694-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harring described her interpretation after seeing the film: "When I saw it the first time, I thought it was the story of Hollywood dreams, illusion and obsession. It touches on the idea that nothing is quite as it seems, especially the idea of being a Hollywood movie star. The second and third times I saw it, I thought it dealt with identity. Do we know who we are? And then I kept seeing different things in itÂ ... There's no right or wrong to what someone takes away from it or what they think the film is really about. It's a movie that makes you continuously ponder, makes you ask questions. I've heard over and over, 'This is a movie that I'll see again' or 'This is a movie you've got to see again.' It intrigues you. You want to get it, but I don't think it's a movie to be gotten. It's achieved its goal if it makes you ask questions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romantic_content">Romantic content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Romantic content">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Treatment of the relationships between Betty and Rita and Diane and Camilla varied between those who were honestly touched by their sincerity and those who were titillated. A review of the film by <i><a href="/wiki/Premiere_(magazine)" title="Premiere (magazine)">Premiere</a></i> states that the relationship between Betty and Rita is "possibly the healthiest, most positive amorous relationship ever depicted in a Lynch movie",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenny_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-kenny-39">[39]</a></sup> while Thierry Jousse, in his review for <i><a href="/wiki/Cahiers_du_cin%C3%A9ma" title="Cahiers du cinÃ©ma">Cahiers du cinÃ©ma</a></i>, notes that the love between these two women is "of lyricism practically without equal in contemporary cinema".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Another points out that the pivotal romantic interlude between Betty and Rita is so poignant and tender by Betty's "understanding for the first time, with self-surprise, that all her helpfulness and curiosity about the other woman had a point: desireÂ ... It is a beautiful moment, made all the more miraculous by its earned tenderness, and its distances from anything lurid."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lopate_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-lopate-41">[41]</a></sup> Another review states the scene's "eroticism is so potent it blankets the whole movie, coloring every scene that came before and every one that follows".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zacharek_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-zacharek-42">[42]</a></sup> Betty and Rita were chosen by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_Film_Channel" title="Independent Film Channel">Independent Film Channel</a> as the emblematic romantic couple of the 2000s. Writer Charles Taylor states, "Betty and Rita are often framed against darkness so soft and velvety it's like a hovering <a href="/wiki/Halo_(religious_iconography)" title="Halo (religious iconography)">nimbus</a>, ready to swallow them if they awake from the film's dream. And when they are swallowed, when smoke fills the frame as if the sulfur of hell itself were obscuring our vision, we feel as if not just a romance has been broken, but the beauty of the world has been cursed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The co-dependency in the relationship between Betty and Ritaâwhich borders on outright obsessionâhas been compared to female relationships in two similar films, <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_(1966_film)" title="Persona (1966 film)">Persona</a></i> (1966) and <i><a href="/wiki/3_Women" title="3 Women">3 Women</a></i> (1977), which also depict identities of vulnerable women that become tangled, interchanging and ultimately merge: "The female couples also mirror each other, with their mutual interactions conflating hero(ine) worship with same-sex desire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFilippo201374_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFilippo201374-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>An analysis of the film in terms of the lesbian as a tragic figure notes the media response to the film: "reviewers rhapsodized in particular and at length about the film's sex scenes, as if there were a contest to see who could enjoy this representation of female same-sex desire the most."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-love_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-love-45">[45]</a></sup> The author, Heather Love, writes that the film used a classic theme in literature and film depicting lesbian relationships: Camilla as achingly beautiful and available, rejecting Diane for Adam. Popular reaction to the film suggests the contrasting relationships between Betty and Rita and Diane and Camilla are "understood as both the hottest thing on earth and, at the same time, as something fundamentally sad and not at all erotic" as "the heterosexual order asserts itself with crushing effects for the abandoned woman".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-love_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-love-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Media portrayals of Naomi Watts' and Laura Elena Harring's views of their onscreen relationships were varied and conflicting. Watts said of the filming of the scene, "I don't see it as erotic, though maybe it plays that way. The last time I saw it, I actually had tears in my eyes because I knew where the story was going. It broke my heart a little bit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, in another interview Watts stated, "I was amazed how honest and real all this looks on screen. These girls look really in love and it was curiously erotic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pearce_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-pearce-21">[21]</a></sup> While Harring was quoted saying, "The love scene just happened in my eyes. Rita's very grateful for the help Betty's given [her] so I'm saying goodbye and goodnight to her, thank you, from the bottom of my heart, I kiss her and then there's just an energy that takes us [over]. Of course I have amnesia so I don't know if I've done it before, but I don't think we're really lesbians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ferber_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ferber-47">[47]</a></sup> Heather Love agreed somewhat with Harring's perception when she stated that identity in <i>Mulholland Drive</i> is not as important as desire: "who we are does not count for muchâwhat matters instead is what we are about to do, what we want to do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-love_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-love-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characters">Characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BettyMulholland.jpg"><img alt="Naomi Watts beaming and facing into soft light holding the arm of an older woman while they take a down escalator at Los Angeles International Airport" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="500" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/BettyMulholland.jpg/300px-BettyMulholland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e2/BettyMulholland.jpg/450px-BettyMulholland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e2/BettyMulholland.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BettyMulholland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Betty (Watts) arrives in Los Angeles; pictured with Irene (<a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Bates" title="Jeanne Bates">Jeanne Bates</a>). Betty is bright and optimistic, in contrast with Watts' portrayal of Diane in the later part of the film.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Betty Elms</b> (<a href="/wiki/Naomi_Watts" title="Naomi Watts">Naomi Watts</a>) is the bright and talented newcomer to Los Angeles, described as "wholesome, optimistic, determined to take the town by storm",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lopate_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-lopate-41">[41]</a></sup> and "absurdly naÃ¯ve".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubin_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-taubin-48">[48]</a></sup> Her perkiness and intrepid approach to helping Rita because it is the right thing to do is reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Drew" title="Nancy Drew">Nancy Drew</a> for reviewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubin_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-taubin-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebert_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebert-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004155_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004155-50">[50]</a></sup> Her entire persona at first is an apparent clichÃ© of small-town naÃ¯vetÃ©. But it is Betty's identity, or loss of it, that appears to be the focus of the film. For one critic, Betty performed the role of the film's consciousness and unconscious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubin_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-taubin-48">[48]</a></sup> Naomi Watts, who modeled Betty on <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day" title="Doris Day">Doris Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tippi_Hedren" title="Tippi Hedren">Tippi Hedren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Novak" title="Kim Novak">Kim Novak</a>, observed that Betty is a thrill-seeker, someone "who finds herself in a world she doesn't belong in and is ready to take on a new identity, even if it's somebody else's".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-17">[17]</a></sup> This has also led one theorist to conclude that since Betty had naÃ¯vely, yet eagerly entered the Hollywood system, she had become a "complicit actor" who had "embraced the very structure that" destroyed her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hageman_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-hageman-28">[28]</a></sup> In an explanation of her development of the Betty character, Watts stated:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>I had to therefore come up with my own decisions about what this meant and what this character was going through, what was dream and what was reality. My interpretation could end up being completely different, from both David and the audience. But I <i>did</i> have to reconcile all of that, and people seem to think it works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Betty, however difficult to believe as her character is established, shows an astonishing depth of dimension in her audition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vass_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-vass-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toles_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-toles-52">[52]</a></sup> Previously rehearsed with Rita in the apartment, where Rita feeds her lines woodenly, the scene is "dreck"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapote_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-lapote-24">[24]</a></sup> and "hollow; every line unworthy of a genuine actress's commitment", and Betty plays it in rehearsal as poorly as it is written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toles_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-toles-52">[52]</a></sup> Nervous but plucky as ever at the audition, Betty enters the cramped room, but when pitted inches from her audition partner (Chad Everett), she turns it into a scene of powerful sexual tension that she fully controls and draws in every person in the room. The sexuality erodes immediately as the scene ends and she stands before them shyly waiting for their approval. One film analyst asserts that Betty's previously unknown ability steals the show, specifically, taking the dark mystery away from Rita and assigning it to herself, and by Lynch's use of this scene illustrates his use of deception in his characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toles_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-toles-52">[52]</a></sup> Betty's acting ability prompts Ruth Perlmutter to speculate if Betty is acting the role of Diane in either a dream or a parody of a film that ultimately turns against her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Rita</b> (<a href="/wiki/Laura_Harring" title="Laura Harring">Laura Elena Harring</a>) is the mysterious and helpless apparent victim, a classic femme fatale with her dark, strikingly beautiful appearance. Roger Ebert was so impressed with Harring that he said of her "all she has to do is stand there and she is the first good argument in 55Â years for a <i><a href="/wiki/Gilda" title="Gilda">Gilda</a></i> remake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebert_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-ebert-49">[49]</a></sup> She serves as the object of desire, directly oppositional to Betty's bright self-assuredness. She is also the first character with whom the audience identifies, and as viewers know her only as confused and frightened, not knowing who she is and where she is going, she represents their desire to make sense of the film through her identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007198_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007198-53">[53]</a></sup> Instead of threatening, she inspires Betty to nurture, console and help her. Her amnesia makes her a blank persona, which one reviewer notes is "the vacancy that comes with extraordinary beauty and the onlooker's willingness to project any combination of angelic and devilish onto her".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lopate_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-lopate-41">[41]</a></sup> A character analysis of Rita asserts that her actions are the most genuine of the first portion of the film, since she has no memory and nothing to use as a frame of reference for how to behave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudson_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-23">[23]</a></sup> Todd McGowan, however, author of a book on themes in Lynch's films, states that the first portion of <i>Mulholland Drive</i> can be construed as Rita's fantasy, until Diane Selwyn is revealed; Betty is the object that overcomes Rita's anxiety about her loss of identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007199_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007199-54">[54]</a></sup> According to film historian Steven Dillon, Diane transitions a former roommate into Rita: following a tense scene where the roommate collects her remaining belongings, Rita appears in the apartment, smiling at Diane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon200694_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon200694-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rita_Poster_Mulholland.jpg"><img alt="Laura Elena Harring wet from a shower and wrapped in a red towel, looking into the mirror at a reflection of the theatrical poster for the film Gilda" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="400" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/Rita_Poster_Mulholland.jpg/300px-Rita_Poster_Mulholland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8e/Rita_Poster_Mulholland.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rita_Poster_Mulholland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The dark-haired woman (Harring) assumes the name "Rita" after seeing the name on a poster. Her search for her identity has been interpreted by film scholars as representing the audience's desire to make sense of the film.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Betty and Rita find the decomposing body, they flee the apartment and their images are split apart and reintegrated. David Roche notes that Rita's lack of identity causes a breakdown that "occurs not only at the level of the character but also at the level of the image; the shot is subjected to special effects that fragment their image and their voices are drowned out in reverb, the camera seemingly writing out the mental state of the characters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roche_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roche-29">[29]</a></sup> Immediately they return to Betty's aunt's apartment where Rita dons a blonde wigâostensibly to disguise herselfâbut making her look remarkably like Betty. It is this transformation that one film analyst suggests is the melding of both identities. This is supported by visual clues, like particular camera angles making their faces appear to be merging into one. This is further illustrated soon after by their sexual intimacy, followed by Rita's personality becoming more dominant as she insists they go to Club Silencio at 2 a.m., that eventually leads to the total domination by Camilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Diane Selwyn</b> (Naomi Watts) is the palpably frustrated and depressed woman, who seems to have ridden the coattails of Camilla, whom she idolizes and adores, but who does not return her affection. She is considered to be the reality of the too-good-to-be-true Betty, or a later version of Betty after living too long in Hollywood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holden_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-holden-30">[30]</a></sup> For Steven Dillon, the plot of the film "makes Rita the perfect empty vessel for Diane's fantasies", but because Rita is only a "blank cover girl" Diane has "invested herself in emptiness", which leads her to depression and apparently to suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon200695_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon200695-55">[55]</a></sup> Hence, Diane is the personification of dissatisfaction, painfully illustrated when she is unable to climax while masturbating, in a scene that indicates "through blurred, jerky, point of view shots of the stony wallânot only her tears and humiliation but the disintegration of her fantasy and her growing desire for revenge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sinnerbrink_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-sinnerbrink-27">[27]</a></sup> One analysis of Diane suggests her devotion to Camilla is based on a manifestation of <a href="/wiki/Narcissism" title="Narcissism">narcissism</a>, as Camilla embodies everything Diane wants and wants to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridgway_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ridgway-56">[56]</a></sup> Although she is portrayed as weak and the ultimate loser, for Jeff Johnson, author of a book about morality in Lynch films, Diane is the only character in the second portion of the film whose moral code remains intact. She is "a decent person corrupted by the miscellaneous miscreants who populate the film industry".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004137_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004137-57">[57]</a></sup> Her guilt and regret are evident in her suicide, and in the clues that surface in the first portion of the film. Rita's fear, the dead body and the illusion at Club Silencio indicate that something is dark and wrong in Betty and Rita's world. In becoming free from Camilla, her moral conditioning kills her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004137.E2.80.93138_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson2004137.E2.80.93138-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Camilla Rhodes</b> (<a href="/wiki/Melissa_George" title="Melissa George">Melissa George</a>, Laura Elena Harring) is little more than a face in a photo and a name that has inspired many representatives of some vaguely threatening power to place her in a film against the wishes of Adam. Referred to as a "vapid moll" by one reviewer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fullerbabes_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-fullerbabes-59">[59]</a></sup> she barely makes an impression in the first portion of the film, but after the blue box is opened and she is portrayed by Laura Elena Harring, she becomes a full person who symbolizes "betrayal, humiliation and abandonment",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lapote_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-lapote-24">[24]</a></sup> and is the object of Diane's frustration. Diane is a sharp contrast to Camilla, who is more voluptuous than ever, and who appears to have "sucked the life out of Diane".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-love_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-love-45">[45]</a></sup> Immediately after telling Diane that she drives her wild, Camilla tells her they must end their affair. On a film set where Adam is directing Camilla, he orders the set cleared, except for Dianeâat Camilla's requestâwhere Adam shows another actor just how to kiss Camilla correctly. Instead of punishing Camilla for such public humiliation, as is suggested by Diane's conversation with the bungling hit man, one critic views Rita as the vulnerable representation of Diane's desire for Camilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wyman_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-wyman-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Adam Kesher</b> (<a href="/wiki/Justin_Theroux" title="Justin Theroux">Justin Theroux</a>) is established in the first portion of the film as a "vaguely arrogant",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000208_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000208-61">[61]</a></sup> but apparently successful, director who endures one humiliation after another. Theroux said of his role, "He's sort of the one character in the film who doesn't know what the [hell's] going on. I think he's the one guy the audience says, 'I'm kind of like you right now. I don't know why you're being subjected to all this pain.<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neman_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-neman-13">[13]</a></sup> After being stripped of creative control of his film, he is <a href="/wiki/Cuckold" title="Cuckold">cuckolded</a> by the pool cleaner (played by <a href="/wiki/Billy_Ray_Cyrus" title="Billy Ray Cyrus">Billy Ray Cyrus</a>), and thrown out of his own opulent house above Hollywood. After he checks into a seedy motel and pays with cash, the manager arrives to tell him that his credit is no good. Witnessed by Diane, Adam is pompous and self-important. He is the only character whose personality does not seem to change completely from the first part of the film to the second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007205.E2.80.93206_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007205.E2.80.93206-62">[62]</a></sup> One analysis of Adam's character contends that because he capitulated and chose Camilla Rhodes for his film, that is the end of Betty's cheerfulness and ability to help Rita, placing the blame for her tragedy on the representatives of studio power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Minor characters include The Cowboy (<a href="/wiki/Monty_Montgomery_(producer)" title="Monty Montgomery (producer)">Monty Montgomery</a>, credited as Lafayette Montgomery), the Castigliani Brothers (<a href="/wiki/Dan_Hedaya" title="Dan Hedaya">Dan Hedaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Badalamenti" title="Angelo Badalamenti">Angelo Badalamenti</a>) and Mr. Roque (<a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Anderson" title="Michael J. Anderson">Michael J. Anderson</a>), all of whom are somehow involved in pressuring Adam to cast Camilla Rhodes in his film. These characters represent the death of creativity for film scholars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridgway_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ridgway-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheenDavison2004171_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheenDavison2004171-63">[63]</a></sup> and they portray a "vision of the industry as a closed hierarchical system in which the ultimate source of power remains hidden behind a series of representatives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubin_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-taubin-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ann_Miller" title="Ann Miller">Ann Miller</a> portrays Coco, the landlady who welcomes Betty to her wonderful new apartment. Coco, in the first part of the film, represents the old guard in Hollywood, who welcomes and protects Betty. In the second part of the film, however, she appears as Adam's mother, who impatiently chastises Diane for being late to the party and barely pays attention to Diane's embarrassed tale of how she got into acting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridgway_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-ridgway-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mulholland_Drive_Mr_Roque.jpg"><img alt="A short, strange-looking man seated in a large wooden wheelchair under an intense beam of light in a large and sparsely furnished room; a desk is in a far corner and the walls are covered in curtains." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="400" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Mulholland_Drive_Mr_Roque.jpg/300px-Mulholland_Drive_Mr_Roque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/66/Mulholland_Drive_Mr_Roque.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mulholland_Drive_Mr_Roque.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dwarf actor <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Anderson" title="Michael J. Anderson">Michael J. Anderson</a>, as Mr. Roque, was fitted with oversized prosthetic limbs in order to give him the appearance of an abnormally small head.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The filmmaking style of David Lynch has been written about extensively using descriptions like "ultraweird",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zacharek_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-zacharek-42">[42]</a></sup> "dark"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup> and "oddball".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson20046_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson20046-64">[64]</a></sup> Todd McGowan writes, "One cannot watch a Lynch film the way one watches a standard Hollywood film noir nor in the way that one watches most radical films."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGowan20072_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGowan20072-65">[65]</a></sup> Through Lynch's juxtaposition of clichÃ© and surreal, nightmares and fantasies, nonlinear story lines, camera work, sound and lighting, he presents a film that challenges viewers to suspend belief of what they are experiencing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vass_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-vass-34">[34]</a></sup> Many of the characters in <i>Mulholland Drive</i> are archetypes that can only be perceived as clichÃ©: the new Hollywood hopeful, the femme fatale, the maverick director and shady powerbrokers that Lynch never seems to explore fully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-love_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-love-45">[45]</a></sup> Lynch places these often hackneyed characters in dire situations, creating dream-like qualities. By using these characters in scenarios that have components and references to dreams, fantasies and nightmares, viewers are left to decide, between the extremes, what is reality. One film analyst writes of him, "Like most surrealists, Lynch's language of the unexplained is the fluid language of dreams."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudson_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>David Lynch uses various methods of deception in <i>Mulholland Drive</i>. A shadowy figure named Mr. Roque, who seems to control film studios, is portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism">dwarf</a> actor <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Anderson" title="Michael J. Anderson">Michael J. Anderson</a> (also from <i><a href="/wiki/Twin_Peaks" title="Twin Peaks">Twin Peaks</a></i>). Anderson, who has only two lines and is seated in an enormous wooden wheelchair, was fitted with oversized foam <a href="/wiki/Prosthesis" title="Prosthesis">prosthetic</a> arms and legs in order to portray his head as abnormally small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000209_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000209-66">[66]</a></sup> During Adam and Camilla's party, Diane watches Camilla (played by Harring) with Adam on one arm, lean over and deeply kiss the same woman who appeared as Camilla (Melissa George) before the blue box was opened. Both then turn and smile pointedly at Diane. Film critic Franklin Ridgway writes that the depiction of such a deliberate "cruel and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_manipulation" title="Psychological manipulation">manipulative</a>" act makes it unclear if Camilla is as capricious as she seems, or if Diane's paranoia is allowing the audience only to see what she senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridgway_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-ridgway-56">[56]</a></sup> In a scene immediately after Betty's audition, the film cuts to a woman singing without apparent accompaniment, but as the camera pulls backwards, the audience sees that it is a recording studio. In actuality, it is a sound stage where Betty has just arrived to meet Adam Kesher, that the audience realizes as the camera pulls back further. Ridgway insists that such deception through artful camera work sets the viewer full of doubt about what is being presented: "It is as if the camera, in its graceful fluidity of motion, reassures us that it (thinks it) sees everything, has everything under control, even if we (and Betty) do not."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridgway_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-ridgway-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Stephen Dillon, Lynch's use of different camera positions throughout the film, such as hand-held points of view, makes the viewer "identify with the suspense of the character in his or her particular space", but that Lynch at moments also "disconnects the camera from any particular point of view, thereby ungrounding a single or even a human perspective" so that the multiple perspectives keep contexts from merging, significantly troubling "our sense of the individual and the human".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon2006100_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon2006100-67">[67]</a></sup> Andrew Hageman similarly notes that the camera work in the film "renders a very disturbing sense of place and presence", such as the scene in Winkie's where the "camera floats irregularly during the shot-reverse shot dialogue" by which the "spectator becomes aware that a set of normally objective shots have become disturbingly subjective".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hageman_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-hageman-28">[28]</a></sup> Scholar Curt Hersey recognizes several <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> techniques used in the film including lack of transitions, abrupt transitions, motion speed, nontraditional camera movement, <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated imagery</a>, nondiegetic images, <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_narrative" title="Nonlinear narrative">nonlinear narration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intertextuality" title="Intertextuality">intertextuality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:286px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FightMulholland.jpg"><img alt="Naomi Watts and Laura Elena Harring arguing on two sides of an open door" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="175" data-file-width="284" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6c/FightMulholland.jpg" width="284"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FightMulholland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An emotionally troubled Diane exchanges words with Camilla. Diane's scenes were characterized by different lighting to symbolize her physical and spiritual impoverishment.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first portion of the film that establishes the characters of Betty, Rita and Adam presents some of the most logical filmmaking of Lynch's career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hudson_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccarthy_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccarthy-69">[69]</a></sup> The later part of the film that represents reality to many viewers, however, exhibits a marked change in cinematic effect that gives it a quality just as surreal as the first part. Diane's scenes feature choppier editing and dirtier lighting that symbolize her physical and spiritual impoverishment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup> which contrasts with the first portion of the film where "even the plainest decor seems to sparkle", Betty and Rita glow with light and transitions between scenes are smooth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgowan_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgowan-70">[70]</a></sup> Lynch moves between scenes in the first portion of the film by using panoramic shots of the mountains, palm trees and buildings in Los Angeles. In the darker part of the film, sound transitions to the next scene without a visual reference where it is taking place. At Camilla's party, when Diane is most humiliated, the sound of crashing dishes is heard that carries immediately to the scene where dishes have been dropped in the diner, and Diane is speaking with the hit man. Sinnerbrink also notes that several scenes in the film, such as the one featuring Diane's hallucination of Camilla after Diane wakes up, the image of the being from behind Winkie's after Diane's suicide, or the "repetition, reversal and displacement of elements that were differently configured" in the early portion of the film, creates the <a href="/wiki/Uncanny" title="Uncanny">uncanny effect</a> where viewers are presented with familiar characters or situations in altered times or locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sinnerbrink_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-sinnerbrink-27">[27]</a></sup> Similarly, Hageman has identified the early scene at Winkie's as "extremely uncanny", because it is a scene where the "boundaries separating physical reality from the imaginary realities of the unconscious disintegrate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hageman_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-hageman-28">[28]</a></sup> Author Valtteri Kokko has identified three groups of "uncanny metaphors": the <a href="/wiki/Doppelg%C3%A4nger" title="DoppelgÃ¤nger">doppelgÃ¤nger</a> of multiple characters played by the same actors, dreams and an everyday objectâprimarily the blue boxâthat initiates Rita's disappearance and Diane's real life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another recurring element in Lynch's films is his experimentation with sound. He stated in an interview, "you look at the image and the scene silent, it's doing the job it's supposed to do, but the work isn't done. When you start working on the sound, keep working until it feels correct. There's so many wrong sounds and instantly you know it. Sometimes it's really magical."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divine_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-divine-7">[7]</a></sup> In the opening scene of the film, as the dark-haired woman stumbles off Mulholland Drive, silently it suggests she is clumsy. After Lynch added "a hint of the steam [from the wreck] and the screaming kids", however, it transformed Laura Elena Harring from clumsy to terrified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoods2000208_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoods2000208-61">[61]</a></sup> Lynch also infused subtle rumblings throughout portions of the film that reviewers noted added unsettling and creepy effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horan_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-horan-72">[72]</a></sup> Hageman also identifies "perpetual and uncanny ambient sound", and places a particular emphasis on the scene where the man collapses behind Winkie's as normal sound is drowned out by a buzzing roar, noting that the noise "creates a dissonance and suspense that draws in the spectator as detective to place the sound and reestablish order".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hageman_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-hageman-28">[28]</a></sup> <i>Mulholland Drive</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s ending with the woman at Club Silencio whispering is an example of Lynch's aural deception and surreality, according to Ruth Perlmutter, who writes, "The acting, the dreams, the search for identity, the fears and terrors of the undefined self are over when the film is over, and therefore, there is only silence and enigma."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perlmutter_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-perlmutter-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Soundtrack">Soundtrack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mulholland_Drive_(film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Soundtrack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The album progresses much like a typical Lynch film, opening with a quick, pleasant Jitterbug and then slowly delving into darker string passages, the twangy guitar sounds of '50s diner music and, finally, the layered, disturbing, often confusing underbelly of the score.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Neil Shurley, 2002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The soundtrack of <i>Mulholland Drive</i> was supervised by <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Badalamenti" title="Angelo Badalamenti">Angelo Badalamenti</a>, who collaborated on previous Lynch projects <i>Blue Velvet</i> and <i>Twin Peaks</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jolin_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-jolin-74">[74]</a></sup> Badalamenti, who was nominated for awards from the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> (AFI) and the <a href="/wiki/British_Academy_of_Film_and_Television_Arts" title="British Academy of Film and Television Arts">British Academy of Film and Television Arts</a> (BAFTA) for his work on the film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afi_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-afi-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bafta_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-bafta-76">[76]</a></sup> also has a cameo as an espresso aficionado and mobster.</p>
<p>Reviewers note that Badalamenti's ominous score, described as his "darkest yet",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norelli_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norelli-77">[77]</a></sup> contributes to the sense of mystery as the film opens on the dark-haired woman's limousine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007197_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGowan2007197-78">[78]</a></sup> that contrasts with the bright, hopeful tones of Betty's first arrival in Los Angeles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jolin_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-jolin-74">[74]</a></sup> with the score "acting as an emotional guide for the viewer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norelli_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Norelli-77">[77]</a></sup> Film music journalist Daniel Schweiger remarks that Badalamenti's contribution to the score alternates from the "nearly motionless string dread to noir jazz and <a href="/wiki/Audio_feedback" title="Audio feedback">audio feedback</a>", with "the rhythms building to an explosion of infinite darkness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweiger_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweiger-79">[79]</a></sup> Badalamenti described a particular technique of sound design applied to the film, by which he would provide Lynch with multiple ten- to twelve-minute tracks at slow tempo, that they called "firewood",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweiger_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schweiger-79">[79]</a></sup> from which Lynch "would take fragments and experiment with them resulting in a lot of film's eerie soundscapes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norelli_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Norelli-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lynch uses two pop songs from the 1960s directly after one another, playing as two actresses are auditioning by <a href="/wiki/Lip_sync" title="Lip sync">lip synching</a> them. According to an analyst of music used in Lynch films, Lynch's female characters are often unable to communicate through normal channels and are reduced to lip-synching or being otherwise stifled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazullo_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-mazullo-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Connie_Stevens" title="Connie Stevens">Connie Stevens</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Sixteen_Reasons" title="Sixteen Reasons">Sixteen Reasons</a>" is the song being sung while the camera pans backwards to reveal several illusions, and <a href="/wiki/Linda_Scott" title="Linda Scott">Linda Scott</a>'s version of "<a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Told_Ev%27ry_Little_Star" title="I've Told Ev'ry Little Star">I've Told Ev'ry Little Star</a>" is the audition for the first Camilla Rhodes, that film scholar Eric Gans considers a song of empowerment for Betty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gans_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-gans-81">[81]</a></sup> Originally written by <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a> as a duet, sung by Linda Scott in this rendition by herself, Gans suggests it takes on a homosexual overtone in <i>Mulholland Drive</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gans_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-gans-81">[81]</a></sup> Unlike "Sixteen Reasons", however, portions of "I've Told Ev'ry Little Star" are distorted to suggest "a sonic split-identity" for Camilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazullo_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-mazullo-80">[80]</a></sup> When the song plays, Betty has just entered the sound stage where Adam is auditioning actresses for his film, and she sees Adam, locks eyes with him and abruptly flees after Adam has declared "This is the girl" about Camilla, thereby avoiding his inevitable rejection.</p>
<p>At the hinge of the film is a scene in an unusual late night theater called Club Silencio where a performer announces "No hay banda (there is no band)... but yet we hear a band", variated between English, Spanish and French. Described as "the most original and stunning sequence in an original and stunning film",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nochimson_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-nochimson-35">[35]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebekah_del_Rio" title="Rebekah del Rio">Rebekah del Rio</a>'s Spanish <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> rendition of "<a href="/wiki/Crying_(Roy_Orbison_song)" title="Crying (Roy Orbison song)">Crying</a>", named "Llorando", is praised as "show-stoppingÂ ... except that there's no show to stop" in the sparsely attended Club Silencio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubin_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-taubin-48">[48]</a></sup> Lynch wanted to use <a href="/wiki/Roy_Orbison" title="Roy Orbison">Roy Orbison</a>'s version of "Crying" in <i>Blue Velvet</i>, but changed his mind when he heard Orbison's "<a href="/wiki/In_Dreams_(Roy_Orbison_song)" title="In Dreams (Roy Orbison song)">In Dreams</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macaulay_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-macaulay-3">[3]</a></sup> Del Rio, who popularized the Spanish version and who received her first recording contract on the basis of the song, stated that Lynch flew to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nashville" title="Nashville">Nashville</a> where she was living, and she sang the song for him once and did not know he was recording her. Lynch wrote a part for her in the film and used the version she sang for him in Nashville.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The song tragically serenades the lovers Betty and Rita, who sit spellbound and weeping, moments before their relationship disappears and is replaced by Diane and Camilla's dysfunction.
